Rev. 361 (1996) (criticizing the Loftin study). And, of course, the District\xe2\x80\x99s amici produce counter-rejoinders, referring to articles that defend their studies. See, e.g., APHA Brief 23, n.\xc2\xa05 (citing McDowall, Loftin, & Wiersema et\xc2\xa0al., Using Quasi-Experiments to Evaluate Firearm Laws, 30 Law & Soc. Rev. 381 (1996)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The upshot is a set of studies and counterstudies that, at most, could leave a judge uncertain about the proper policy conclusion. But from respondent\xe2\x80\x99s perspective any such uncertainty is not good enough. That is because legislators, not judges, have primary responsibility for drawing policy conclusions from empirical fact. And, given that constitutional allocation of decisionmaking responsibility, the empirical evidence presented here is sufficient to allow a judge to reach a firm legal conclusion.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In particular this Court, in First Amendment cases applying intermediate scrutiny, has said that our \xe2\x80\x9csole obligation\xe2\x80\x9d in reviewing a legislature\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cpredictive judgments\xe2\x80\x9d is \xe2\x80\x9cto assure that, in formulating its judgments,\xe2\x80\x9d the legislature \xe2\x80\x9chas drawn reasonable inferences based on substantial evidence.\xe2\x80\x9d Turner, 520 U. S., at 195 (internal quotation marks omitted). And judges, looking at the evidence before us, should agree that the District legislature\xe2\x80\x99s predictive judgments satisfy that legal standard. That is to say, the District\xe2\x80\x99s judgment, while open to question, is nevertheless supported by \xe2\x80\x9csubstantial evidence.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is no cause here to depart from the standard set forth in Turner, for the District\xe2\x80\x99s decision represents the kind of empirically based judgment that legislatures, not courts, are best suited to make. See Nixon, 528 U. S., at 402 (Breyer, J., concurring). In fact, deference to legislative judgment seems particularly appropriate here, where the judgment has been made by a local legislature, with particular knowledge of local problems and insight into appropriate local solutions. See Los Angeles v. Alameda Books, Inc., 535 U. S. 425, 440 (2002) (plurality opinion) (\xe2\x80\x9c[W]e must acknowledge that the Los Angeles City Council is in a better position than the Judiciary to gather an evaluate data on local problems\xe2\x80\x9d); cf. DC Rep., at 67 (statement of Rep. Gude) (describing District\xe2\x80\x99s law as \xe2\x80\x9ca decision made on the local level after extensive debate and deliberations\xe2\x80\x9d). Different localities may seek to solve similar problems in different ways, and a \xe2\x80\x9ccity must be allowed a reasonable opportunity to experiment with solutions to admittedly serious problems.\xe2\x80\x9d Renton v. Playtime Theatres, Inc., 475 U. S. 41, 52 (1986) (internal quotation marks omitted). \xe2\x80\x9cThe Framers recognized that the most effective democracy occurs at local levels of government, where people with firsthand knowledge of local problems have more ready access to public officials responsible for dealing with them.\xe2\x80\x9d Garcia v. San Antonio Metropolitan Transit Authority, 469 U. S. 528, 575, n. 18 (1985) (Powell, J., dissenting) (citing The Federalist No. 17, p. 107 (J. Cooke ed. 1961) (A. Hamilton)). We owe that democratic process some substantial weight in the constitutional calculus.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For these reasons, I conclude that the District\xe2\x80\x99s statute properly seeks to further the sort of life-preserving and public-safety interests that the Court has called \xe2\x80\x9ccompelling.\xe2\x80\x9d Salerno, 481 U. S., at 750, 754.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I next assess the extent to which the District\xe2\x80\x99s law burdens the interests that the Second Amendment seeks to protect. Respondent and his amici, as well as the majority, suggest that those interests include: (1) the preservation of a \xe2\x80\x9cwell regulated Militia\xe2\x80\x9d; (2) safeguarding the use of firearms for sporting purposes, e.g., hunting and marksmanship; and (3) assuring the use of firearms for self-defense. For argument\xe2\x80\x99s sake, I shall consider all three of those interests here.

\n

1

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The District\xe2\x80\x99s statute burdens the Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s first and primary objective hardly at all. As previously noted, there is general agreement among the Members of the Court that the principal (if not the only) purpose of the Second Amendment is found in the Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s text: the preservation of a \xe2\x80\x9cwell regulated Militia.\xe2\x80\x9d See supra, at 3. What scant Court precedent there is on the Second Amendment teaches that the Amendment was adopted \xe2\x80\x9c[w]ith obvious purpose to assure the continuation and render possible the effectiveness of [militia] forces\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cmust be interpreted and applied with that end in view.\xe2\x80\x9d Miller, 307 U. S., at 178. Where that end is implicated only minimally (or not at all), there is substantially less reason for constitutional concern. Compare ibid. (\xe2\x80\x9cIn the absence of any evidence tending to show that possession or use of a \xe2\x80\x98shotgun having a barrel of less than eighteen inches in length\xe2\x80\x99 at this time has some reasonable relationship to the preservation or efficiency of a well\nregulated militia, we cannot say that the Second Amendment guarantees the right to keep and bear such an\ninstrument\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To begin with, the present case has nothing to do with actual military service. The question presented presumes that respondent is \xe2\x80\x9cnot affiliated with any state-regulated militia.\xe2\x80\x9d 552 U. S. __ (2007) (emphasis added). I am aware of no indication that the District either now or in the recent past has called up its citizenry to serve in a militia, that it has any inkling of doing so anytime in the foreseeable future, or that this law must be construed to prevent the use of handguns during legitimate militia activities. Moreover, even if the District were to call up its militia, respondent would not be among the citizens whose service would be requested. The District does not consider him, at 66 years of age, to be a member of its militia. See D.\xc2\xa0C. Code \xc2\xa749\xe2\x80\x93401 (2001) (militia includes only male residents ages 18 to 45); App. to Pet. for Cert. 120a (indicating respondent\xe2\x80\x99s date of birth).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nonetheless, as some amici claim, the statute might interfere with training in the use of weapons, training useful for military purposes. The 19th-century constitutional scholar, Thomas Cooley, wrote that the Second Amendment protects \xe2\x80\x9clearning to handle and use [arms] in a way that makes those who keep them ready for their efficient use\xe2\x80\x9d during militia service. General Principles of Constitutional Law 271 (1880); ante, at 45 (opinion of the Court); see also ante, at 45\xe2\x80\x9346 (citing other scholars agreeing with Cooley on that point). And former military officers tell us that \xe2\x80\x9cprivate ownership of firearms makes for a more effective fighting force\xe2\x80\x9d because \xe2\x80\x9c[m]ilitary recruits with previous firearms experience and training are generally better marksmen, and accordingly, better soldiers.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Retired Military Officers as Amici Curiae 1\xe2\x80\x932 (hereinafter Military Officers\xe2\x80\x99 Brief). An amicus brief filed by retired Army generals adds that a \xe2\x80\x9cwell-regulated militia\xe2\x80\x94whether ad\xc2\xa0hoc or as part of our organized military\xe2\x80\x94depends on recruits who have familiarity and training with firearms\xe2\x80\x94rifles, pistols, and shotguns.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Major General John D. Altenburg, Jr., et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 4 (hereinafter Generals\xe2\x80\x99 Brief). Both briefs point out the importance of handgun training. Military Officers\xe2\x80\x99 Brief 26\xe2\x80\x9328; Generals\xe2\x80\x99 Brief 4. Handguns are used in military service, see id., at 26, and \xe2\x80\x9ccivilians who are familiar with handgun marksmanship and safety are much more likely to be able to safely and accurately fire a rifle or other firearm with minimal training upon entering military service,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 28.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Regardless, to consider the military-training objective a modern counterpart to a similar militia-related colonial objective and to treat that objective as falling within the Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s primary purposes makes no difference here. That is because the District\xe2\x80\x99s law does not seriously affect military training interests. The law permits residents to engage in activities that will increase their familiarity with firearms. They may register (and thus possess in their homes) weapons other than handguns, such as rifles and shotguns. See D. C. Code \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa77\xe2\x80\x932502.01, 7\xe2\x80\x932502.02(a) (only weapons that cannot be registered are sawed-off shotguns, machine guns, short-barreled rifles, and pistols not registered before 1976); compare Generals\xe2\x80\x99 Brief 4 (listing \xe2\x80\x9crifles, pistols, and shotguns\xe2\x80\x9d as useful military weapons; emphasis added). And they may operate those weapons within the District \xe2\x80\x9cfor lawful recreational purposes.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa77\xe2\x80\x932507.02; see also \xc2\xa77\xe2\x80\x932502.01(b)(3) (nonresidents \xe2\x80\x9cparticipating in any lawful recreational firearm-related activity in the District, or on his way to or from such activity in another jurisdiction\xe2\x80\x9d may carry even weapons not registered in the District). These permissible recreations plainly include actually using and firing the weapons, as evidenced by a specific D. C. Code provision contemplating the existence of local firing ranges. See\n\xc2\xa77\xe2\x80\x932507.03.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0And while the District law prevents citizens from training with handguns within the District, the District consists of only 61.4 square miles of urban area. See Dept. of Commerce, Bureau of Census, United States: 2000 (pt. 1), p. 11 (2002) (Table 8). The adjacent States do permit the use of handguns for target practice, and those States are only a brief subway ride away. See Md. Crim. Law Code Ann. \xc2\xa74\xe2\x80\x93203(b)(4) (Lexis Supp. 2007) (general handgun restriction does not apply to \xe2\x80\x9cthe wearing, carrying, or transporting by a person of a handgun used in connection with,\xe2\x80\x9d inter alia, \xe2\x80\x9ca target shoot, formal or informal target practice, sport shooting event, hunting, [or] a Department of Natural Resources-sponsored firearms and hunter safety class\xe2\x80\x9d); Va. Code Ann. \xc2\xa718.2\xe2\x80\x93287.4 (Lexis Supp. 2007) (general restriction on carrying certain loaded pistols in certain public areas does not apply \xe2\x80\x9cto any person actually engaged in lawful hunting or lawful recreational shooting activities at an established shooting range or shooting contest\xe2\x80\x9d); Washington Metropolitan Area Transit Authority, Metrorail System Map, http://www.wmata.com/\nmetrorail/systemmmap.cfm.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Of course, a subway rider must buy a ticket, and the ride takes time. It also costs money to store a pistol, say, at a target range, outside the District. But given the costs already associated with gun ownership and firearms training, I cannot say that a subway ticket and a short subway ride (and storage costs) create more than a minimal burden. Compare Crawford v. Marion County Election Bd., 553 U. S. ___, ___ (2008) (slip op., at 3) (Breyer, J., dissenting) (acknowledging travel burdens on indigent persons in the context of voting where public transportation options were limited). Indeed, respondent and two of his coplaintiffs below may well use handguns outside the District on a regular basis, as their declarations indicate that they keep such weapons stored there. See App. to Pet. for Cert. 77a (respondent); see also id., at 78a, 84a (coplaintiffs). I conclude that the District\xe2\x80\x99s law burdens the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s primary objective little, or not at all.

\n

2

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority briefly suggests that the \xe2\x80\x9cright to keep and bear Arms\xe2\x80\x9d might encompass an interest in hunting. See, e.g., ante, at 26. But in enacting the present provisions, the District sought \xe2\x80\x9cto take nothing away from sportsmen.\xe2\x80\x9d DC Rep., at\xc2\xa033. And any inability of District residents to hunt near where they live has much to do with the jurisdiction\xe2\x80\x99s exclusively urban character and little to do with the District\xe2\x80\x99s firearm laws. For reasons similar to those I discussed in the preceding subsection\xe2\x80\x94that the District\xe2\x80\x99s law does not prohibit possession of rifles or shotguns, and the presence of opportunities for sporting activities in nearby States\xe2\x80\x94I reach a similar conclusion, namely, that the District\xe2\x80\x99s law burdens any sports-related or hunting-related objectives that the Amendment may protect little, or not at all.

\n

3

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The District\xe2\x80\x99s law does prevent a resident from keeping a loaded handgun in his home. And it consequently makes it more difficult for the householder to use the handgun for self-defense in the home against intruders, such as burglars. As the Court of Appeals noted, statistics suggest that handguns are the most popular weapon for self defense. See 478 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 400 (citing Kleck & Gertz, 86 J. Crim. L. & C., at 182\xe2\x80\x93183). And there are some legitimate reasons why that would be the case: Amici suggest (with some empirical support) that handguns are easier to hold and control (particularly for persons with physical infirmities), easier to carry, easier to maneuver in enclosed spaces, and that a person using one will still have a hand free to dial 911. See ILEETA Brief 37\xe2\x80\x9339; NRA Brief 32\xe2\x80\x9333; see also ante, at 57. But see Brief for Petitioners 54\xe2\x80\x9355 (citing sources preferring shotguns and rifles to handguns for purposes of self-defense). To that extent the law burdens to some degree an interest in self-defense that for present purposes I have assumed the Amendment seeks to further.

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In weighing needs and burdens, we must take account of the possibility that there are reasonable, but less restrictive alternatives. Are there other potential measures that might similarly promote the same goals while imposing lesser restrictions? See Nixon, 528 U. S., at 402 (Breyer, J., concurring) (\xe2\x80\x9cexistence of a clearly superior, less restrictive alternative\xe2\x80\x9d can be a factor in determining whether a law is constitutionally proportionate). Here I see none.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The reason there is no clearly superior, less restrictive alternative to the District\xe2\x80\x99s handgun ban is that the ban\xe2\x80\x99s very objective is to reduce significantly the number of handguns in the District, say, for example, by allowing a law enforcement officer immediately to assume that any handgun he sees is an illegal handgun. And there is no plausible way to achieve that objective other than to ban the guns.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It does not help respondent\xe2\x80\x99s case to describe the District\xe2\x80\x99s objective more generally as an \xe2\x80\x9ceffort to diminish the dangers associated with guns.\xe2\x80\x9d That is because the very attributes that make handguns particularly useful for self-defense are also what make them particularly dangerous. That they are easy to hold and control means that they are easier for children to use. See Brief for American Academy of Pediatrics et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 19 (\xe2\x80\x9c[C]hildren as young as three are able to pull the trigger of most handguns\xe2\x80\x9d). That they are maneuverable and permit a free hand likely contributes to the fact that they are by far the firearm of choice for crimes such as rape and robbery. See Weapon Use and Violent Crime 2 (Table 2). That they are small and light makes them easy to steal, see supra, at 19, and concealable, cf. ante, at 54 (opinion of the Court) (suggesting that concealed-weapon bans are constitutional).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This symmetry suggests that any measure less restrictive in respect to the use of handguns for self-defense will, to that same extent, prove less effective in preventing the use of handguns for illicit purposes. If a resident has a handgun in the home that he can use for self-defense, then he has a handgun in the home that he can use to commit suicide or engage in acts of domestic violence. See supra, at 18 (handguns prevalent in suicides); Brief for National Network to End Domestic Violence et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 27 (handguns prevalent in domestic violence). If it is indeed the case, as the District believes, that the number of guns contributes to the number of gun-related crimes, accidents, and deaths, then, although there may be less restrictive, less effective substitutes for an outright ban, there is no less restrictive equivalent of an outright ban.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Licensing restrictions would not similarly reduce the handgun population, and the District may reasonably fear that even if guns are initially restricted to law-abiding citizens, they might be stolen and thereby placed in the hands of criminals. See supra, at 19. Permitting certain types of handguns, but not others, would affect the commercial market for handguns, but not their availability. And requiring safety devices such as trigger locks, or imposing safe-storage requirements would interfere with any self-defense interest while simultaneously leaving operable weapons in the hands of owners (or others capable of acquiring the weapon and disabling the safety device) who might use them for domestic violence or other crimes.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The absence of equally effective alternatives to a complete prohibition finds support in the empirical fact that other States and urban centers prohibit particular types of weapons. Chicago has a law very similar to the District\xe2\x80\x99s, and many of its suburbs also ban handgun possession under most circumstances. See Chicago, Ill., Municipal Code \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa78\xe2\x80\x9320\xe2\x80\x93030(k), 8\xe2\x80\x9320\xe2\x80\x9340, 8\xe2\x80\x9320\xe2\x80\x9350(c) (2008); Evanston, Ill., City Code \xc2\xa79\xe2\x80\x938\xe2\x80\x932 (2007); Morton Grove, Ill., Village Code \xc2\xa76\xe2\x80\x932\xe2\x80\x933(C) (2008); Oak Park, Ill., Village Code \xc2\xa727\xe2\x80\x932\xe2\x80\x931 (2007); Winnetka, Ill., Village Ordinance \xc2\xa79.12.020(B) (2008); Wilmette, Ill., Ordinance \xc2\xa712\xe2\x80\x9324(b) (2008). Toledo bans certain types of handguns. Toledo, Ohio, Municipal Code, ch. 549.25 (2007). And San Francisco in 2005 enacted by popular referendum a ban on most handgun possession by city residents; it has been precluded from enforcing that prohibition, however, by state-court decisions deeming it pre-empted by state law. See Fiscal v. City and County of San Francisco, 158 Cal. App. 4th 895, 900\xe2\x80\x93901, 70 Cal. Rptr. 3d 324, 326\xe2\x80\x93328 (2008). (Indeed, the fact that as many as 41 States may pre-empt local gun regulation suggests that the absence of more regulation like the District\xe2\x80\x99s may perhaps have more to do with state law than with a lack of locally perceived need for them. See Legal Community Against Violence, Regulating Guns in America 14 (2006), http://www.\nlcav.org/Library/reports_analyses/National_Audit_Total_\n8.16.06.pdf.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition, at least six States and Puerto Rico impose general bans on certain types of weapons, in particular assault weapons or semiautomatic weapons. See Cal. Penal Code \xc2\xa712280(b) (West Supp. 2008); Conn. Gen. Stat. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa753\xe2\x80\x93202c (2007); Haw. Rev. Stat. \xc2\xa7134\xe2\x80\x938 (1993); Md. Crim. Law Code Ann. \xc2\xa74\xe2\x80\x93303(a) (Lexis 2002); Mass. Gen. Laws, ch. 140, \xc2\xa7131M (West 2006); N.\xc2\xa0Y. Penal Law Ann. \xc2\xa7265.02(7) (West Supp. 2008); 25 Laws P.\xc2\xa0R. Ann. \xc2\xa7456m (Supp. 2006); see also 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7922(o) (federal machinegun ban). And at least 14 municipalities do the same. See Albany, N.\xc2\xa0Y., Municipal Code \xc2\xa7193\xe2\x80\x9316(A) (2005); Aurora, Ill., Ordinance \xc2\xa729\xe2\x80\x9349(a) (2007); Buffalo, N.\xc2\xa0Y., City Code \xc2\xa7180\xe2\x80\x931(F) (2000); Chicago, Ill., Municipal Code \xc2\xa78\xe2\x80\x9324\xe2\x80\x93025(a), 8\xe2\x80\x9320\xe2\x80\x93030(h); Cincinnati, Ohio, Admin. Code \xc2\xa7708\xe2\x80\x9337(a) (Supp. 2008); Cleveland, Ohio, Ordinance \xc2\xa7628.03(a) (2008); Columbus, Ohio, City Code \xc2\xa72323.31 (2007); Denver, Colo., Municipal Code \xc2\xa738\xe2\x80\x93130(e) (2008); Morton Grove, Ill., Village Code \xc2\xa76\xe2\x80\x932\xe2\x80\x933(B); N.\xc2\xa0Y.\xc2\xa0C. Admin. Code \xc2\xa710\xe2\x80\x93303.1 (2007); Oak Park, Ill., Village Code \xc2\xa727\xe2\x80\x932-1; Rochester, N.\xc2\xa0Y., Code \xc2\xa747\xe2\x80\x935(f) (2008); South Bend, Ind., Ordinance \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa713\xe2\x80\x9397(b), 13\xe2\x80\x9398 (2008); Toledo, Ohio, Municipal Code \xc2\xa7549.23(a). These bans, too, suggest that there may be no substitute to an outright prohibition in cases where a governmental body has deemed a particular type of weapon especially dangerous.

\n

D

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The upshot is that the District\xe2\x80\x99s objectives are compelling; its predictive judgments as to its law\xe2\x80\x99s tendency to achieve those objectives are adequately supported; the law does impose a burden upon any self-defense interest that the Amendment seeks to secure; and there is no clear less restrictive alternative. I turn now to the final portion of the \xe2\x80\x9cpermissible regulation\xe2\x80\x9d question: Does the District\xe2\x80\x99s law disproportionately burden Amendment-protected interests? Several considerations, taken together, convince me that it does not.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, the District law is tailored to the life-threatening problems it attempts to address. The law concerns one class of weapons, handguns, leaving residents free to possess shotguns and rifles, along with ammunition. The area that falls within its scope is totally urban. Cf. Lorillard Tobacco Co. v. Reilly, 533 U. S. 525, 563 (2001) (varied effect of statewide speech restriction in \xe2\x80\x9crural, urban, or suburban\xe2\x80\x9d locales \xe2\x80\x9cdemonstrates a lack of narrow tailoring\xe2\x80\x9d). That urban area suffers from a serious handgun-fatality problem. The District\xe2\x80\x99s law directly aims at that compelling problem. And there is no less restrictive way to achieve the problem-related benefits that it seeks.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, the self-defense interest in maintaining loaded handguns in the home to shoot intruders is not the primary interest, but at most a subsidiary interest, that the Second Amendment seeks to serve. The Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s language, while speaking of a \xe2\x80\x9cMilitia,\xe2\x80\x9d says nothing of \xe2\x80\x9cself-defense.\xe2\x80\x9d As Justice Stevens points out, the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s drafting history shows that the language reflects the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 primary, if not exclusive, objective. See ante, at 17\xe2\x80\x9328 (dissenting opinion). And the majority itself says that \xe2\x80\x9cthe threat that the new Federal Government would destroy the citizens\xe2\x80\x99 militia by taking away their arms was the reason that right . . . was codified in a written Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 26 (emphasis added). The way in which the Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s operative clause seeks to promote that interest\xe2\x80\x94by protecting a right \xe2\x80\x9cto keep and bear Arms\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94may in fact help further an interest in self-defense. But a factual connection falls far short of a primary objective. The Amendment itself tells us that militia preservation was first and foremost in the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 minds. See Miller, 307 U. S., at 178 (\xe2\x80\x9cWith obvious purpose to assure the continuation and render possible the effectiveness of [militia] forces the declaration and guarantee of the Second Amendment were made,\xe2\x80\x9d and the amendment \xe2\x80\x9cmust be interpreted and applied with that end in view\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Further, any self-defense interest at the time of the Framing could not have focused exclusively upon urban-crime related dangers. Two hundred years ago, most Americans, many living on the frontier, would likely have thought of self-defense primarily in terms of outbreaks of fighting with Indian tribes, rebellions such as Shays\xe2\x80\x99 Rebellion, marauders, and crime-related dangers to travelers on the roads, on footpaths, or along waterways. See Dept. of Commerce, Bureau of Census, Population: 1790 to 1990 (1998) (Table 4), online at http://www.census.gov/\npopulation/censusdata/table-4.pdf (of the 3,929,214 Americans in 1790, only 201,655\xe2\x80\x94about 5%\xe2\x80\x94lived in urban areas). Insofar as the Framers focused at all on the tiny fraction of the population living in large cities, they would have been aware that these city dwellers were subject to firearm restrictions that their rural counterparts were not. See supra, at 4\xe2\x80\x937. They are unlikely then to have thought of a right to keep loaded handguns in homes to confront intruders in urban settings as central. And the subsequent development of modern urban police departments, by diminishing the need to keep loaded guns nearby in case of intruders, would have moved any such right even further away from the heart of the amendment\xe2\x80\x99s more basic protective ends. See, e.g., Sklansky, The Private Police, 46 UCLA L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1165, 1206\xe2\x80\x931207 (1999) (professional urban police departments did not develop until roughly the mid-19th century).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nor, for that matter, am I aware of any evidence that handguns in particular were central to the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 conception of the Second Amendment. The lists of militia-related weapons in the late 18th-century state statutes appear primarily to refer to other sorts of weapons, muskets in particular. See Miller, 307 U. S., at 180\xe2\x80\x93182 (reproducing colonial militia laws). Respondent points out in his brief that the Federal Government and two States at the time of the founding had enacted statutes that listed handguns as \xe2\x80\x9cacceptable\xe2\x80\x9d militia weapons. Brief for Respondent 47. But these statutes apparently found them \xe2\x80\x9cacceptable\xe2\x80\x9d only for certain special militiamen (generally, certain soldiers on horseback), while requiring muskets or rifles for the general infantry. See Act of May 8, 1792, ch. XXXIII, 1 Stat. 271; Laws of the State of North Carolina 592 (1791); First Laws of the State of Connecticut 150 (1784); see also 25 Journals of the Continental Congress, pp. 1774\xe2\x80\x931789 741\xe2\x80\x93742 (1922).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Third, irrespective of what the Framers could have thought, we know what they did think. Samuel Adams, who lived in Boston, advocated a constitutional amendment that would have precluded the Constitution from ever being \xe2\x80\x9cconstrued\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cprevent the people of the United States, who are peaceable citizens, from keeping their own arms.\xe2\x80\x9d 6 Documentary History of the Ratification of the Constitution 1453 (J. Kaminski & G. Saladino eds. 2000). Samuel Adams doubtless knew that the Massachusetts Constitution contained somewhat similar protection. And he doubtless knew that Massachusetts law prohibited Bostonians from keeping loaded guns in the house. So how could Samuel Adams have advocated such protection unless he thought that the protection was consistent with local regulation that seriously impeded urban residents from using their arms against intruders? It seems unlikely that he meant to deprive the Federal Government of power (to enact Boston-type weapons regulation) that he know Boston had and (as far as we know) he would have thought constitutional under the Massachusetts Constitution. Indeed, since the District of Columbia (the subject of the Seat of Government Clause, U. S. Const., Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl. 17) was the only urban area under direct federal control, it seems unlikely that the Framers thought about urban gun control at all. Cf. Palmore v. United States, 411 U. S. 389, 397\xe2\x80\x93398 (1973) (Congress can \xe2\x80\x9clegislate for the District in a manner with respect to subjects that would exceed its powers, or at least would be very unusual, in the context of national legislation enacted under other powers delegated to it\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Of course the District\xe2\x80\x99s law and the colonial Boston law are not identical. But the Boston law disabled an even wider class of weapons (indeed, all firearms). And its existence shows at the least that local legislatures could impose (as here) serious restrictions on the right to use firearms. Moreover, as I have said, Boston\xe2\x80\x99s law, though highly analogous to the District\xe2\x80\x99s, was not the only colonial law that could have impeded a homeowner\xe2\x80\x99s ability to shoot a burglar. Pennsylvania\xe2\x80\x99s and New York\xe2\x80\x99s laws could well have had a similar effect. See supra, at 6\xe2\x80\x937. And the Massachusetts and Pennsylvania laws were not only thought consistent with an unwritten common-law gun-possession right, but also consistent with written state constitutional provisions providing protections similar to those provided by the Federal Second Amendment. See supra, at 6\xe2\x80\x937. I cannot agree with the majority that these laws are largely uninformative because the penalty for violating them was civil, rather than criminal. Ante, at 61\xe2\x80\x9362. The Court has long recognized that the exercise of a constitutional right can be burdened by penalties far short of jail time. See, e.g., Murdock v. Pennsylvania, 319 U. S. 105 (1943) (invalidating $7 per week solicitation fee as applied to religious group); see also Forsyth County v. Nationalist Movement, 505 U. S. 123, 136 (1992) (\xe2\x80\x9cA tax based on the content of speech does not become more constitutional because it is a small tax\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Regardless, why would the majority require a precise colonial regulatory analogue in order to save a modern gun regulation from constitutional challenge? After all, insofar as we look to history to discover how we can constitutionally regulate a right to self-defense, we must look, not to what 18th-century legislatures actually did enact, but to what they would have thought they could enact. There are innumerable policy-related reasons why a legislature might not act on a particular matter, despite having the power to do so. This Court has \xe2\x80\x9cfrequently cautioned that it is at best treacherous to find in congressional silence alone the adoption of a controlling rule of law.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Wells, 519 U. S. 482, 496 (1997). It is similarly \xe2\x80\x9ctreacherous\xe2\x80\x9d to reason from the fact that colonial legislatures did not enact certain kinds of legislation an unalterable constitutional limitation on the power of a modern legislature cannot do so. The question should not be whether a modern restriction on a right to self-defense duplicates a past one, but whether that restriction, when compared with restrictions originally thought possible, enjoys a similarly strong justification. At a minimum that similarly strong justification is what the District\xe2\x80\x99s modern law, compared with Boston\xe2\x80\x99s colonial law, reveals.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Fourth, a contrary view, as embodied in today\xe2\x80\x99s decision, will have unfortunate consequences. The decision will encourage legal challenges to gun regulation throughout the Nation. Because it says little about the standards used to evaluate regulatory decisions, it will leave the Nation without clear standards for resolving those challenges. See ante, at 54, and n. 26. And litigation over the course of many years, or the mere specter of such litigation, threatens to leave cities without effective protection against gun violence and accidents during that time.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As important, the majority\xe2\x80\x99s decision threatens severely to limit the ability of more knowledgeable, democratically elected officials to deal with gun-related problems. The majority says that it leaves the District \xe2\x80\x9ca variety of tools for combating\xe2\x80\x9d such problems. Ante, at 64. It fails to list even one seemingly adequate replacement for the law it strikes down. I can understand how reasonable individuals can disagree about the merits of strict gun control as a crime-control measure, even in a totally urbanized area. But I cannot understand how one can take from the elected branches of government the right to decide whether to insist upon a handgun-free urban populace in a city now facing a serious crime problem and which, in the future, could well face environmental or other emergencies that threaten the breakdown of law and order.

\n

V

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority derides my approach as \xe2\x80\x9cjudge-empowering.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 62. I take this criticism seriously, but I do not think it accurate. As I have previously explained, this is an approach that the Court has taken in other areas of constitutional law. See supra, at 10\xe2\x80\x9311. Application of such an approach, of course, requires judgment, but the very nature of the approach\xe2\x80\x94requiring careful identification of the relevant interests and evaluating the law\xe2\x80\x99s effect upon them\xe2\x80\x94limits the judge\xe2\x80\x99s choices; and the method\xe2\x80\x99s necessary transparency lays bare the judge\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning for all to see and to criticize.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority\xe2\x80\x99s methodology is, in my view, substantially less transparent than mine. At a minimum, I find it difficult to understand the reasoning that seems to underlie certain conclusions that it reaches.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority spends the first 54 pages of its opinion attempting to rebut Justice Stevens\xe2\x80\x99 evidence that the Amendment was enacted with a purely militia-related purpose. In the majority\xe2\x80\x99s view, the Amendment also protects an interest in armed personal self-defense, at least to some degree. But the majority does not tell us precisely what that interest is. \xe2\x80\x9cPutting all of [the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s] textual elements together,\xe2\x80\x9d the majority says, \xe2\x80\x9cwe find that they guarantee the individual right to possess and carry weapons in case of confrontation.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 19. Then, three pages later, it says that \xe2\x80\x9cwe do not read the Second Amendment to permit citizens to carry arms for any sort of confrontation.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 22. Yet, with one critical exception, it does not explain which confrontations count. It simply leaves that question unanswered.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority does, however, point to one type of confrontation that counts, for it describes the Amendment as \xe2\x80\x9celevat[ing] above all other interests the right of law-abiding, responsible citizens to use arms in defense of hearth and home.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 63. What is its basis for finding that to be the core of the Second Amendment right? The only historical sources identified by the majority that even appear to touch upon that specific matter consist of an 1866 newspaper editorial discussing the Freedmen\xe2\x80\x99s Bureau Act, see ante, at 43, two quotations from that 1866 Act\xe2\x80\x99s legislative history, see ante, at 43\xe2\x80\x9344, and a 1980 state court opinion saying that in colonial times the same were used to defend the home as to maintain the militia, see ante, at 52. How can citations such as these support the far-reaching proposition that the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s primary concern is not its stated concern about the militia, but rather a right to keep loaded weapons at one\xe2\x80\x99s bedside to shoot intruders?

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nor is it at all clear to me how the majority decides which loaded \xe2\x80\x9carms\xe2\x80\x9d a homeowner may keep. The majority says that that Amendment protects those weapons \xe2\x80\x9ctypically possessed by law-abiding citizens for lawful purposes.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 53. This definition conveniently excludes machineguns, but permits handguns, which the majority describes as \xe2\x80\x9cthe most popular weapon chosen by Americans for self-defense in the home.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 57; see also ante, at 54\xe2\x80\x9355. But what sense does this approach make? According to the majority\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning, if Congress and the States lift restrictions on the possession and use of machineguns, and people buy machineguns to protect their homes, the Court will have to reverse course and find that the Second Amendment does, in fact, protect the individual self-defense-related right to possess a machinegun. On the majority\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning, if tomorrow someone invents a particularly useful, highly dangerous self-defense weapon, Congress and the States had better ban it immediately, for once it becomes popular Congress will no longer possess the constitutional authority to do so. In essence, the majority determines what regulations are permissible by looking to see what existing regulations permit. There is no basis for believing that the Framers intended such circular reasoning.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I am similarly puzzled by the majority\xe2\x80\x99s list, in Part III of its opinion, of provisions that in its view would survive Second Amendment scrutiny. These consist of (1) \xe2\x80\x9cprohibitions on carrying concealed weapons\xe2\x80\x9d; (2) \xe2\x80\x9cprohibitions on the possession of firearms by felons\xe2\x80\x9d; (3) \xe2\x80\x9cprohibitions on the possession of firearms by \xe2\x80\xa6 the mentally ill\xe2\x80\x9d; (4) \xe2\x80\x9claws forbidding the carrying of firearms in sensitive places such as schools and government buildings\xe2\x80\x9d; and (5) government \xe2\x80\x9cconditions and qualifications\xe2\x80\x9d attached \xe2\x80\x9cto the commercial sale of arms.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 54. Why these? Is it that similar restrictions existed in the late 18th century? The majority fails to cite any colonial analogues. And even were it possible to find analogous colonial laws in respect to all these restrictions, why should these colonial laws count, while the Boston loaded-gun restriction (along with the other laws I have identified) apparently does not count? See supra, at 5\xe2\x80\x936, 38\xe2\x80\x9339.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At the same time the majority ignores a more important question: Given the purposes for which the Framers enacted the Second Amendment, how should it be applied to modern-day circumstances that they could not have anticipated? Assume, for argument\xe2\x80\x99s sake, that the Framers did intend the Amendment to offer a degree of self-defense protection. Does that mean that the Framers also intended to guarantee a right to possess a loaded gun near swimming pools, parks, and playgrounds? That they would not have cared about the children who might pick up a loaded gun on their parents\xe2\x80\x99 bedside table? That they (who certainly showed concern for the risk of fire, see supra, at 5\xe2\x80\x937) would have lacked concern for the risk of accidental deaths or suicides that readily accessible loaded handguns in urban areas might bring? Unless we believe that they intended future generations to ignore such matters, answering questions such as the questions in this case requires judgment\xe2\x80\x94judicial judgment exercised within a framework for constitutional analysis that guides that judgment and which makes its exercise transparent. One cannot answer those questions by combining inconclusive historical research with judicial ipse dixit.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The argument about method, however, is by far the less important argument surrounding today\xe2\x80\x99s decision. Far more important are the unfortunate consequences that today\xe2\x80\x99s decision is likely to spawn. Not least of these, as I have said, is the fact that the decision threatens to throw into doubt the constitutionality of gun laws throughout the United States. I can find no sound legal basis for launching the courts on so formidable and potentially dangerous a mission. In my view, there simply is no untouchable constitutional right guaranteed by the Second Amendment to keep loaded handguns in the house in crime-ridden urban areas.

\n

VI

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For these reasons, I conclude that the District\xe2\x80\x99s measure is a proportionate, not a disproportionate, response to the compelling concerns that led the District to adopt it. And, for these reasons as well as the independently sufficient reasons set forth by Justice Stevens, I would find the District\xe2\x80\x99s measure consistent with the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s demands.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0With respect, I dissent.

\n
\n
